Distinguishing the Three Proposition Types by Subject Existence
Author classifies propositions into three types by subject existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given proposition is kharijiyya, haqiqiyya, or dhihniyya based on the existence of its subject's individuals.

Core Idea
Propositions vary by existence: external, assumed, or mental.

In logic, the truth of a proposition often depends on the existence of its subject. This passage from the Tadhheeb classifies propositions into three types based on whether the subject's individuals exist in the external world, are merely assumed, or exist only in the mind. Understanding this classification is crucial for evaluating the scope of universal judgments in theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify subject and predicate
Find the subject (موضوع) and predicate (محمول) in the proposition. The subject is what the judgment is about.
2
Determine existential status of subject individuals
Ask: Do the individuals of the subject actually exist in the external world? If not, can they be assumed to exist? Or are they impossible to exist and only in the mind?
Use the examples: 'every human' (existent), 'every phoenix' (assumed), 'partner of the Creator' (impossible, mental).
3
Classify the proposition
If individuals exist externally → kharijiyya. If assumed existence (possible but not actual) → haqiqiyya. If only mental existence (impossible external) → dhihniyya.
The judgment for kharijiyya applies to both existing and assumed individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
ثمَّ إِنْ لم يكن أفرادُ (ج) موجودةٌ في الخارج؛ فالحكم مقصور على الأفراد المقدَّرةِ الوجودِ ، كقولنا : «كلّ عنقاء طائرُ» •
وإن كانت موجودة في الخارج ؛ فالحكم ليس مقصوراً على أفراده الموجودة في الخارج، بل عليها وعلى أفرادِه المقدَّرةِ الوجودِ أيضاً، كقولنا : كلَّ إنسان حيوان».
وإِمَّا ألا يكون على الأفراد الموجودة في الخارج ولا المقدَّرة فيه، بل على الأفراد الموجودة في الذهن فقط، وهي القضيَّةُ الذهنيَّةُ، كَقولنا : شريك الباري (معدوم، فإنَّ أفراد الموضوع ليست موجودةً في الخارج
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : شَرِيْكُ الْبَارِي ... إِلَخ) أي: كلُّ مَا فرضَهُ العقلُ شريكاً للباري، فهو يمتنع في الخارج.
العطار
و كذا نفس الأمر، بل يكتفي بمجرَّدِ فرض صدقِهِ أو إمكانِ فرض صدقِهِ عليهِ كَمَا في صدقِ الكُلِّيّ على جُزئيَّاتِهِ، حتَّى إذا وقعَ الكُلِّيُّ موضوع القضيَّةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ؛ كانَ مُتناولاً لجميع أفرادِهِ التي هو كُلّيّ بالقياس إليها ؛ كُلّي بالقياس إليها ؛ سواء أمكن صدقه عليها أَوْ لَا، وأمَّا إِذا اعتبر إمكانَ صدقِ الوصف العنواني على ذاتِ الموضوع في نفس الأمرِ كَما هو مذهبُ الفارابي ؛ أو اعتبر مع الإمكانِ الصِّدقَ بالفعلِ كَمَا هو مَذهَبُ الشَّيخِ؛ فَلَا حاجة إلى اعتبار إمكان وجودِ الأفراد، والمحذور مندفع
(قَوْلُهُ : وهي الْقَضِيَّةُ الذَّهْنِيَّةُ) لم يذكرْهَا صاحبُ الشَّمسيَّة؛ لأنَّها غيرُ مُعتبرة في العلوم، والمقصود ضبط القضايا المستعملة فيها غالباً، وتلك نادرة الوقوع وَقَولُهم : إِنَّ قواعد الفنّ يجب أن تكونَ عامَّةٌ ؛ يُجابُ : بأنَّ تعميم القواعدِ إِنَّما هو بقدرِ الطَّاقة الإنسانية، والمصنِّفُ ذكرَهَا هُنا استيفاء للأقسام. (قَوْلُهُ : شَرِيْكُ البَارِي (معدومٌ أي : كُلُّ ما فرضَهُ العقلُ شريك الباري ؛ فَهُو مُمتنع في الخارج، ودخل تحتَ الكاف جميعُ القضايا الَّتي مَوضُوعاتُهَا مُمتنعةٌ، فالمحكوم عليه بالامتناع أفراد هذا المفهوم؛ لا هذا المفهوم، فإنَّهُ أمر اعتباري؛ لأَنَّهُ مِن قَبيلِ الكَليَّات .
:

المنطقية
۵۱۸
ولا مقدَّرةً فيه ؛ لعدم إمكانِ التَّقدير، لكن موجودة في الذهن. وإلى كلّ ما ذكرنا مفصَّلاً ؛ أشارَ مُجملاً بقوله : وَلَا بُدَّ فِي الموجِبَةِ مِنْ وُجُودِ المَوْضُوعِ)، إمَّا (مُحَقِّقاً وَهِيَ الخَارِجِيَّةُ، أَوْ مُقَدَّراً
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : لِعَدَمِ إِمْكَانِ التَّقْدِيرِ ) أي : الفرض ؛ أي: لعدم إمكانه إمكاناً صحيحاً، وإلا ، فا ؛ فالتَّقدير ممكن، ولو قالَ : لاستحالة وجودِهَا ؛ لكان أحسن
(قَوْلُهُ : لَكِنَّها مَوْجُوْدَةٌ) أي: هي موجودةٌ .
(قَوْلُهُ : مُفَصَّلَاً حالٌ مِمَّا ذكرنَاهُ . (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ مُقَدَّرَاً) ليس المراد بالمقدَّرِ ؛ ما يُبايِنُ المحقق، بل ما يشمله ويشمل المعدومَ كَمَا أشارَ لهُ الشَّارح.
المطار
(قَوْلُهُ: وَهِيَ الْخَارِجِيَّةُ) أي : تُسمَّى بذلك منسوبةٌ لِلخارج أي : ما هو خارجٌ عَنِ المشاعرِ والقُوى الدَّاركةِ ؛ لأنَّ موضوعها اعتُبِرَ اتِّصافه بالمحمول خارجاً، قال المصنِّفُ في شرح الرسالة : سواء كانَ اتِّصافُهُ بِ(ب) حالَ الحكم أو قبلَهُ أو بعدَهُ؛ حَتَّى يصدقَ : كُلُّ نائم مُستيقظ ، وإنْ لم يكن اتِّصافُهُ بالنَّائم حال ثبوتِ اليقظة؛ فالمراد بالحكمِ هَهُنَا ثبوتُ المحمول للموضُوع أو انتفاؤُهُ عنه؛ لا حكمُ العقل بذلك؛ لأنَّ هذا الكلامَ إنَّما هو لِرَفْعِ تَوَهُم مَنْ ظَنَّ أَنَّ الذَّاتَ يجبُ اتِّصافُهُ بوصفِ الموضوعِ حالَ اتصافه بالمحمول، وهُوَ الَّذي يُسمّيهِ القومُ : حالَ اعتبار الحكم، وإلا ؛ فَفِي حال حكم العقل لا يجب وجود الموضوع في الخارج ؛ فضلاً عن انصافه بالعنوانِ؛ لِصدقِ قولنا : زيد موجود أمس أو غداً .ا.هـ. وقال الهروي : لا يخفى أنَّهُ إذا كانَ المحمول فعلاً أو مُشتقا أو مصدراً ؛ يجب أن يكونَ الذَّاتُ مُتَّصِفَاً بالعنوانِ حينَ ثبوتِ المحمول بحسب قواعدِ اللُّغةِ، وكُلُّ نائم مُستيقظ : لا يصح بحسب حقيقةِ اللُّغةِ؛ تَأَمَّلْ ا.هـ (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ مُقَدَّرَاً) قالَ الجَلالُ : ما معنى قول المصنفِ : إنَّ الحقيقية تقتضي الوجود المقدَّرَ لِلموضوع ؛ والوجود المقدَّرُ لَا حجر فيه، فلا فائدةً في اعتباره؟ قلتُ : إِن اعتُبرَ في موضوع الحقيقية إمكان صدقِ العنوانِ على الأفراد أو إمكان وجودها ؛ فالمراد بالوجودِ المَقدَّرِ : الوجود المقدَّرُ معَ ذلك القيدِ ، ولا يخفى فائدة اعتباره؛ وهي إخراج
Chapter: On the known (subject) and its divisions. Then, if the individuals of (j) are not existent in the external world, the judgment is confined to the assumed individuals of existence, like our saying: 'Every 'Anqa' is a bird.' And if they are existent in the external world, the judgment is not confined to its individuals present in the external world, but rather upon them and upon its assumed individuals of existence as well, like our saying: 'Every human is an animal.' Or it may not be upon the individuals existent in the external world nor those assumed therein, but rather upon the individuals existent in the mind only, and that is the mental proposition, like our saying: 'A partner of the Creator is non-existent,' for the individuals of the subject are not existent in the external world. Al-Dasuqi: His saying: 'A partner of the Creator... etc.' means: every thing that the mind assumes as a partner to the Creator, it is impossible in the external world. Al-Atar: And likewise [consideration of] the actual matter, but rather it suffices with mere assumption of its truth or the possibility of assuming its truth upon it, like the truth of the universal upon its particulars. So when the universal occurs as subject of a universal proposition, it encompasses all its individuals in relation to which it is a universal, whether it is possible for it to be true of them or not. And if one considers the possibility of the truth of the titular description upon the essence of the subject in actual fact as is the doctrine of al-Farabi, or considers along with possibility the actual truth as is the doctrine of the Shaykh, then there is no need to consider the possibility of existence of the individuals, and the problem is obviated. (His saying: 'and that is the mental proposition') - The author of al-Shamsiyya did not mention it because it is not considered in the sciences, and the intent is to survey the propositions used in them predominantly, and that is rare occurrence. And their saying: 'The rules of the art must be general' - it is answered: generalization of rules is only to the extent of human capacity, and the author mentioned it here for completeness of divisions. (His saying: 'A partner of the Creator is non-existent') means: every thing that the mind assumes as a partner of the Creator, it is impossible in the external world; and under the kaf (the example) fall all propositions whose subjects are impossible. So what is judged as impossible are the individuals of this concept, not this concept itself, for it is a mental consideration, because it is of the category of universals. And [it is] not assumed therein due to the impossibility of assumption, but it exists in the mind. And to all that we have mentioned in detail, he pointed summarily with his saying: 'And in the affirmative proposition, there must be existence of the subject,' either (realized, which is the external proposition, or assumed). Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'due to the impossibility of assumption') meaning: assumption; i.e., due to the impossibility of it being a correct possibility. Otherwise, assumption is possible, and if he had said: due to the impossibility of its existence, it would have been better. (His saying: 'but it is existent') means: it is existent. (His saying: 'detailed' is a state from what we have mentioned). (His saying: 'or assumed') - what is meant by 'assumed' is not what is distinct from the realized, but rather what includes it and includes the non-existent as the commentator indicated. Al-Mutar: (His saying: 'which is the external proposition') means: it is so named relative to the external, i.e., what is outside the senses and the perceiving faculties, because its subject is considered to be described by the predicate externally. The author said in his commentary on al-Risala: whether its description by (b) occurs at the time of judgment or before it or after it, so that it is true to say: 'Every sleeper will wake up,' even if his description as sleeper is not present at the time of wakefulness. What is meant by 'judgment' here is the affirmation of the predicate for the subject or its negation from it, not the mind's judgment of that, because this speech is only to remove the illusion of one who thought that the essence must be described by the description of the subject at the time of its description by the predicate, which the people call: the state of considering the judgment. Otherwise, in the state of the mind's judgment, the existence of the subject in the external world is not required, let alone its description by the title; for the truth of our saying: 'Zayd existed yesterday or tomorrow.' End quote. Al-Harawi said: It is not hidden that if the predicate is a verb or a derivative or a verbal noun, the essence must be described by the title at the time of the predicate's affirmation according to the rules of language, and 'Every sleeper will wake up' is not valid according to the reality of language; reflect. End quote. (His saying: 'or assumed') - al-Jalal said: What is the meaning of the author's statement: 'The haqiqiyya requires assumed existence for the subject' while assumed existence is of no consequence, so there is no benefit in considering it? I say: If there is considered in the subject of the haqiqiyya the possibility of the truth of the title upon the individuals or the possibility of their existence, then what is meant by 'assumed existence' is assumed existence with that condition, and the benefit of considering it is not hidden; it is to exclude...
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of propositions based on the existential status of the subject's individuals. It begins with the chapter title 'On the known and its divisions,' where 'the known' refers to the subject of judgment. The author (the matn) presents three cases: First, when the individuals of the subject (represented by the variable ج) do not exist in the external world—such as 'every phoenix is a bird' where phoenixes are imaginary—the judgment applies only to assumed individuals. Second, when the individuals do exist externally—such as 'every human is an animal'—the judgment applies to both existing and assumed individuals. Third, when the individuals are neither externally existent nor even feasible to assume, but exist only in the mind—such as 'a partner of the Creator is non-existent'—this is the mental proposition (qadiyya dhihniyya). The commentaries then elaborate. Al-Dasuqi clarifies that the example 'partner of the Creator' means any imagined partner; the judgment of non-existence refers to the individuals of that concept, not the concept itself, which is a universal mental consideration. Al-Atar (al-Atar) discusses the 'haqiqiyya' (real) proposition, stating that it suffices with the mere assumption of truth of the universal on its particulars, without requiring actual existence. He contrasts the approaches of al-Farabi (who considers possibility of truth) and Ibn Sina (who requires actual truth) to avoid problems. The commentary then notes that the author of al-Shamsiyya omitted the mental proposition because it is rarely used in sciences, but it is included here for completeness. Further comments explain the necessity of subject existence in affirmative propositions: either realized (kharijiyya) or assumed (haqiqiyya). Al-Dasuqi discusses the phrase 'due to impossibility of assumption' suggesting it would be better phrased as 'impossibility of existence.' Al-Mutar defines the external proposition as one where the subject's description by the predicate is considered externally, regardless of temporal coincidence, citing the example 'every sleeper will wake up.' The passage concludes with a discussion from al-Jalal on the benefit of considering assumed existence in haqiqiyya propositions: it excludes impossible subjects but includes those possible in assumption.

Key Terms
القضية الخارجية
القضية الحقيقية
القضية الذهنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which type of proposition is exemplified by 'Every partner of the Creator is non-existent'?
A) Kharijiyya
B) Haqiqiyya
C) Dhihniyya
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The existence status of the subject's individuals determines the type of proposition: external (existent), real (assumed), or mental (only in mind).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following proposition: 'Every possible world is created.' Is it kharijiyya, haqiqiyya, or dhihniyya? Explain your reasoning based on the existence of 'possible worlds.'

The Debate on Including the Mental Proposition
Commentary on why the mental proposition is omitted in some works

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the reasons for including or omitting the mental proposition (qadiyya dhihniyya) from logic textbooks.

Core Idea
The mental proposition is rarely used, but included for completeness.

Although the mental proposition is defined, it is rarely employed in the sciences. The commentator discusses why the author of al-Shamsiyya omitted it and why the present author includes it. This lesson explores the reasoning behind such editorial choices, highlighting the balance between generality and practicality in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The commentator states that the author of al-Shamsiyya did not mention the mental proposition.
Find the sentence: 'لم يذكرْهَا صاحبُ الشَّمسيَّة'.
2
Find the reason for omission
The reason given is that it is not considered in the sciences; the focus is on propositions used predominantly.
The passage says: 'لأنَّها غيرُ مُعتبرة في العلوم'.
3
Understand the justification for inclusion
The present author includes it for completeness of divisions, and the objection that rules must be general is answered by noting that generalization is limited by human capacity.
The commentator responds: 'بأنَّ تعميم القواعد إِنَّما هو بقدرِ الطَّاقة الإنسانية'.

(قَوْلُهُ : لِعَدَمِ إِمْكَانِ التَّقْدِيرِ ) أي : الفرض ؛ أي: لعدم إمكانه إمكاناً صحيحاً، وإلا ، فا ؛ فالتَّقدير ممكن، ولو قالَ : لاستحالة وجودِهَا ؛ لكان أحسن
(قَوْلُهُ : لَكِنَّها مَوْجُوْدَةٌ) أي: هي موجودةٌ .
(قَوْلُهُ : مُفَصَّلَاً حالٌ مِمَّا ذكرنَاهُ . (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ مُقَدَّرَاً) ليس المراد بالمقدَّرِ ؛ ما يُبايِنُ المحقق، بل ما يشمله ويشمل المعدومَ كَمَا أشارَ لهُ الشَّارح.
المطار
(قَوْلُهُ: وَهِيَ الْخَارِجِيَّةُ) أي : تُسمَّى بذلك منسوبةٌ لِلخارج أي : ما هو خارجٌ عَنِ المشاعرِ والقُوى الدَّاركةِ ؛ لأنَّ موضوعها اعتُبِرَ اتِّصافه بالمحمول خارجاً، قال المصنِّفُ في شرح الرسالة : سواء كانَ اتِّصافُهُ بِ(ب) حالَ الحكم أو قبلَهُ أو بعدَهُ؛ حَتَّى يصدقَ : كُلُّ نائم مُستيقظ ، وإنْ لم يكن اتِّصافُهُ بالنَّائم حال ثبوتِ اليقظة؛ فالمراد بالحكمِ هَهُنَا ثبوتُ المحمول للموضُوع أو انتفاؤُهُ عنه؛ لا حكمُ العقل بذلك؛ لأنَّ هذا الكلامَ إنَّما هو لِرَفْعِ تَوَهُم مَنْ ظَنَّ أَنَّ الذَّاتَ يجبُ اتِّصافُهُ بوصفِ الموضوعِ حالَ اتصافه بالمحمول، وهُوَ الَّذي يُسمّيهِ القومُ : حالَ اعتبار الحكم، وإلا ؛ فَفِي حال حكم العقل لا يجب وجود الموضوع في الخارج ؛ فضلاً عن انصافه بالعنوانِ؛ لِصدقِ قولنا : زيد موجود أمس أو غداً .ا.هـ. وقال الهروي : لا يخفى أنَّهُ إذا كانَ المحمول فعلاً أو مُشتقا أو مصدراً ؛ يجب أن يكونَ الذَّاتُ مُتَّصِفَاً بالعنوانِ حينَ ثبوتِ المحمول بحسب قواعدِ اللُّغةِ، وكُلُّ نائم مُستيقظ : لا يصح بحسب حقيقةِ اللُّغةِ؛ تَأَمَّلْ ا.هـ (قَوْلُهُ : أَوْ مُقَدَّرَاً) قالَ الجَلالُ : ما معنى قول المصنفِ : إنَّ الحقيقية تقتضي الوجود المقدَّرَ لِلموضوع ؛ والوجود المقدَّرُ لَا حجر فيه، فلا فائدةً في اعتباره؟ قلتُ : إِن اعتُبرَ في موضوع الحقيقية إمكان صدقِ العنوانِ على الأفراد أو إمكان وجودها ؛ فالمراد بالوجودِ المَقدَّرِ : الوجود المقدَّرُ معَ ذلك القيدِ ، ولا يخفى فائدة اعتباره؛ وهي إخراج
Chapter: On the known (subject) and its divisions. Then, if the individuals of (j) are not existent in the external world, the judgment is confined to the assumed individuals of existence, like our saying: 'Every 'Anqa' is a bird.' And if they are existent in the external world, the judgment is not confined to its individuals present in the external world, but rather upon them and upon its assumed individuals of existence as well, like our saying: 'Every human is an animal.' Or it may not be upon the individuals existent in the external world nor those assumed therein, but rather upon the individuals existent in the mind only, and that is the mental proposition, like our saying: 'A partner of the Creator is non-existent,' for the individuals of the subject are not existent in the external world. Al-Dasuqi: His saying: 'A partner of the Creator... etc.' means: every thing that the mind assumes as a partner to the Creator, it is impossible in the external world. Al-Atar: And likewise [consideration of] the actual matter, but rather it suffices with mere assumption of its truth or the possibility of assuming its truth upon it, like the truth of the universal upon its particulars. So when the universal occurs as subject of a universal proposition, it encompasses all its individuals in relation to which it is a universal, whether it is possible for it to be true of them or not. And if one considers the possibility of the truth of the titular description upon the essence of the subject in actual fact as is the doctrine of al-Farabi, or considers along with possibility the actual truth as is the doctrine of the Shaykh, then there is no need to consider the possibility of existence of the individuals, and the problem is obviated. (His saying: 'and that is the mental proposition') - The author of al-Shamsiyya did not mention it because it is not considered in the sciences, and the intent is to survey the propositions used in them predominantly, and that is rare occurrence. And their saying: 'The rules of the art must be general' - it is answered: generalization of rules is only to the extent of human capacity, and the author mentioned it here for completeness of divisions. (His saying: 'A partner of the Creator is non-existent') means: every thing that the mind assumes as a partner of the Creator, it is impossible in the external world; and under the kaf (the example) fall all propositions whose subjects are impossible. So what is judged as impossible are the individuals of this concept, not this concept itself, for it is a mental consideration, because it is of the category of universals. And [it is] not assumed therein due to the impossibility of assumption, but it exists in the mind. And to all that we have mentioned in detail, he pointed summarily with his saying: 'And in the affirmative proposition, there must be existence of the subject,' either (realized, which is the external proposition, or assumed). Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: 'due to the impossibility of assumption') meaning: assumption; i.e., due to the impossibility of it being a correct possibility. Otherwise, assumption is possible, and if he had said: due to the impossibility of its existence, it would have been better. (His saying: 'but it is existent') means: it is existent. (His saying: 'detailed' is a state from what we have mentioned). (His saying: 'or assumed') - what is meant by 'assumed' is not what is distinct from the realized, but rather what includes it and includes the non-existent as the commentator indicated. Al-Mutar: (His saying: 'which is the external proposition') means: it is so named relative to the external, i.e., what is outside the senses and the perceiving faculties, because its subject is considered to be described by the predicate externally. The author said in his commentary on al-Risala: whether its description by (b) occurs at the time of judgment or before it or after it, so that it is true to say: 'Every sleeper will wake up,' even if his description as sleeper is not present at the time of wakefulness. What is meant by 'judgment' here is the affirmation of the predicate for the subject or its negation from it, not the mind's judgment of that, because this speech is only to remove the illusion of one who thought that the essence must be described by the description of the subject at the time of its description by the predicate, which the people call: the state of considering the judgment. Otherwise, in the state of the mind's judgment, the existence of the subject in the external world is not required, let alone its description by the title; for the truth of our saying: 'Zayd existed yesterday or tomorrow.' End quote. Al-Harawi said: It is not hidden that if the predicate is a verb or a derivative or a verbal noun, the essence must be described by the title at the time of the predicate's affirmation according to the rules of language, and 'Every sleeper will wake up' is not valid according to the reality of language; reflect. End quote. (His saying: 'or assumed') - al-Jalal said: What is the meaning of the author's statement: 'The haqiqiyya requires assumed existence for the subject' while assumed existence is of no consequence, so there is no benefit in considering it? I say: If there is considered in the subject of the haqiqiyya the possibility of the truth of the title upon the individuals or the possibility of their existence, then what is meant by 'assumed existence' is assumed existence with that condition, and the benefit of considering it is not hidden; it is to exclude...
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of propositions based on the existential status of the subject's individuals. It begins with the chapter title 'On the known and its divisions,' where 'the known' refers to the subject of judgment. The author (the matn) presents three cases: First, when the individuals of the subject (represented by the variable ج) do not exist in the external world—such as 'every phoenix is a bird' where phoenixes are imaginary—the judgment applies only to assumed individuals. Second, when the individuals do exist externally—such as 'every human is an animal'—the judgment applies to both existing and assumed individuals. Third, when the individuals are neither externally existent nor even feasible to assume, but exist only in the mind—such as 'a partner of the Creator is non-existent'—this is the mental proposition (qadiyya dhihniyya). The commentaries then elaborate. Al-Dasuqi clarifies that the example 'partner of the Creator' means any imagined partner; the judgment of non-existence refers to the individuals of that concept, not the concept itself, which is a universal mental consideration. Al-Atar (al-Atar) discusses the 'haqiqiyya' (real) proposition, stating that it suffices with the mere assumption of truth of the universal on its particulars, without requiring actual existence. He contrasts the approaches of al-Farabi (who considers possibility of truth) and Ibn Sina (who requires actual truth) to avoid problems. The commentary then notes that the author of al-Shamsiyya omitted the mental proposition because it is rarely used in sciences, but it is included here for completeness. Further comments explain the necessity of subject existence in affirmative propositions: either realized (kharijiyya) or assumed (haqiqiyya). Al-Dasuqi discusses the phrase 'due to impossibility of assumption' suggesting it would be better phrased as 'impossibility of existence.' Al-Mutar defines the external proposition as one where the subject's description by the predicate is considered externally, regardless of temporal coincidence, citing the example 'every sleeper will wake up.' The passage concludes with a discussion from al-Jalal on the benefit of considering assumed existence in haqiqiyya propositions: it excludes impossible subjects but includes those possible in assumption.

Checkpoint
Why did the author of al-Shamsiyya omit the mental proposition according to the passage?
A) It is impossible to define
B) It is rarely used in sciences
C) It is the same as haqiqiyya
D) It was a mistake

Key Takeaway
Inclusion of the mental proposition in logic textbooks is a matter of comprehensiveness, not necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a proposition like 'Every round square is a rectangle.' According to the classification, what type is it? Then, argue whether this proposition should be included in a logic textbook based on the criteria discussed in the commentary.

Propositions
beginner
5 min
Two Types of Existence in Propositions: Judgment vs Predication
Al-Dasuqi explains two aspects of subject existence in a proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the existence required by the judgment and the existence required by the predication in a proposition, and list three differences between them.

Core Idea
Every proposition involves two existences: one for the judgment itself and one for the predication.

In this passage, al-Dasuqi clarifies that the existence of the subject in a proposition can be considered in two distinct ways: from the perspective of the judgment upon it, and from the perspective of the predication belonging to it. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting the truth conditions of propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two existences
Read the passage and locate where al-Dasuqi splits the subject's existence into two: (1) existence from the perspective of the judgment, and (2) existence from the perspective of the predication.
The judgment existence is always mental; the predication existence can be external or mental.
2
List the three differences
Al-Dasuqi mentions three respects in which these two existences differ. Write them down: (i) presence in affirmative vs negative, (ii) duration of consideration, (iii) location (mental vs external/mental).
These differences are stated explicitly in the passage.
3
Apply to a sample proposition
Take the proposition 'The world is originated'. Determine which existence is required for the judgment and which for the predication. Check if the differences hold.
This helps solidify the abstract distinction.

فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
فَالحَقِيقِيَّةُ، أَوْ ذِهْنَاً فَالذِّهْنِيَّةُ).
الدسوقي
والحاصل : أنَّ وجودَ الموضوع تارةً؛ يُعتبرُ مِن حيث الحكم عليه، وتارة؛ يُعتبر من حيث ثبوت المحمول له والوجودُ الَّذي يقتضيه الحكم مخالف للوجود الَّذي يقتضيه ثبوت المحمول للموضوع من أوجه:
الأول : أنَّ الوجود الأوَّلَ يكون في الموجبة والسالبة، والوجود الثاني: لا يكون إلا في الموجبة فقط . الثَّاني من الأوجُهِ : أَنَّ الوجودَ الَّذي يقتضيه الحكمُ إِنَّما يُعتبرُ في حالةِ الحكم فقط؛ بخلافِ الوجودِ الَّذي يقتضيه ثبوتُ المحمول للموضوع، فإِنَّهُ يُعتبرُ دائماً أو ساعة.
الثَّالثُ مِنَ الأومُجهِ : أَنَّ الوجودَ الَّذي يقتضيه الحكم يكون بحسب الذهنِ؛ بخلافِ الوجودِ الَّذي يقتضيه ثبوت المحمول للموضوع، فإنَّه يكون بحسب الخارج تارةً، وبحسب الذهن أُخرى.
قَوْلُهُ : فَالْحَقِيْقَيَّةُ) اعلَمْ أنَّ بين الحقيقيَّةِ والخارجيَّةِ عموماً من وجه، تنفرد الخارجيَّةُ فيما إذا قُلْتَ: كلُّ لون بياضُ، فيما إذا لم يكنْ مِنَ الألوان إلا هو، وتنفرد الحقيقية في: كلُّ عنقاء طائر، ويجتمعان في : كلّ إنسان حيوان، فهي حقيقيَّةٌ باعتبار، وخارجيَّة باعتبار .
العطار
غير الممكنِ مِنَ الممتنعاتِ ؛ وإِنْ لم يعتبرُ كَمَا هو مُقتَضَى كلام بعضِهِم، فَالمراد بالوجودِ المقدَّرِ كونُ الموضوعِ بحيثُ لو وُجِدَ؛ كَانَ مُتَّحداً مع المحمول ا . هـ . قَوْلُهُ : فَالحَقِيقِيَّةُ) سُمِّيَتْ بذلك؛ لأنَّها حقيقةُ القضيَّة المستعملة في العلوم لكثرة استعمالها بهذا الاعتبار، فهو من قبيل نسبةِ الشَّيء إلى مفهومِهِ الَّذي هو كَالحقيقة له، والذهنيَّةُ سُمِّيَتْ بِذلِكَ ؛ لأنَّهُ لَا وجود لموضوعِهَا إِلَّا في الذهنِ، قالَ عبد الحكيم: وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ القضايا الذهنيَّةَ على أقسام ؛ مِنها ما تكونُ أفرادُهَا مَوجودةً في الذهنِ مُتَّصِفَةٌ بمحمولاتها في الذهنِ انصافاً مُطابقاً للواقعِ تَجميعِ المسائل المنطقية؛ فإنَّ محمولاتها عوارض ذهنيَّةً تعرضُ للمعقولات الأولى في الذهن، ويكون لموضوعاتها وُجودانِ ذهنيان؛ أحدهما : مناط الحكم، وهو الوجودُ الظُّلي
وأمَّا النسبة بين الموجود في الخارج والموجود في نَفْسِ الأمرِ؛ فالعموم المطلق؛ لأنَّ كلَّ موجود في الخارج ؛ موجودٌ في نَفْسِ الأمرِ؛ أي : في نَفْسِهِ؛ فهو إظهارٌ في موضع الإضمار ؛ أي : بقطع النَّظرِ عن اعتبار المعتبر وفرض الفارض؛ سواء وجد في الخارج أم لا ؛ فَلِذَا لَا يلزم منه الوجود في الخارج، اجتماعِهِمَا : اللهُ عزَّ وجلَّ، فإنَّهُ مَوجودٌ في الخارج، بحيثُ يجوز رؤيته بالبصر، وفي نَفْسِ الأمرِ بالمعنى المتقدّم، فهذهِ مادَّةُ الاجتماع .
فَمَثَالُ
وأما إمكانُ الحوادث؛ فهو موجودٌ في نَفْسِ الأمر فقط؛ لأنَّهُ لا يُشاهَدُ، وأمَّا النّسبةُ بينَ الموجود في الخارج وفي الدّهنِ؛ فَعموم من وجه، فزيد: يصدقُ عليه
العطار
الَّذِي بِهِ يتغايرُ الموضوعُ والمحمول، وثانيهما: الوجود الأصلي الَّذِي بِهِ اتِّحَادُ المحمول بالموضوع، وهو مناط الصدق والكذب، والفارقُ بين الموجبة والسالبة، ومنها ما تكون محمولاتُها مُستلزمةٌ لِلوجودِ ؛ نحو: شريك الباري ممتنع، واجتماع النَّقيضين مُحالٌ، والمجهول المطلقُ يمتنع الحكم عليه، والمعدوم المطلقُ مُقابل للموجودِ المطلق، وتحقيقه : أنَّ مناط الحكم هو تَصوُّرُها بعنوان الموضوع، ومناط الصدق هو الوجود الفرضيُّ الَّذي باعتبارِ فرديَّةِ الموضوع كأنَّهُ قيلَ: مَا يُتصوَّرُ بعنوان شريكِ الباري ويُفرضُ صدقُهُ عليهِ؛ مُمتنع في نفس الأمرِ، وَقِش على ذلك، ومنها ما تكونُ مَحمولاتُها مُتقدِّمةً على الوجودِ، أو نفس الوجودِ؛ نحو: زيد ممكن أو واجب بالغيرِ أو موجودٌ ؛ فَلِمَوضوعاتِها وجودٌ في الذهنِ حال الحكم كسائر القضايا، وَلِكَوْنِ الاتِّصافِ بها ذهنيّا انتزاعيًّا؛ لا بُدَّ أنْ يكونَ لِمَوضوعاتِها وجودٌ آخَرُ في الذهنِ؛ يكون مبداً لانتزاع هذه الأمور ومناط صدقِ القضيَّةِ واتحاد المحمولات معها ، ثمَّ إذا توجّه العقلُ إليها ولاحظها من حيثُ إِنَّها مَوجودةٌ بهذا الوجودِ؛ انتزع عنها وجوداً وإمكاناً ووجوداً آخر؛ باعتبار الاتصاف بهذا الوجودِ يستدعي تقدم وجود يكون مصداقاً لهذه الأحكام، وليست هذه الملاحظة لازمةٌ للذهن دائماً، فتنقطع بحسب انقطاع الملاحظة ا.هـ. قالَ: وَهوَ مِنَ الغوامض .
Section: On the definition and its divisions.
The real, or mental, then the mental.
Al-Dasuqi:
The summary: That the existence of the subject is sometimes considered from the aspect of the judgment upon it, and sometimes considered from the aspect of the predication belonging to it, and the existence required by the judgment is different from the existence required by the predication belonging to the subject in respects:
First: That the first existence occurs in the affirmative and the negative, while the second existence occurs only in the affirmative alone. Second of the respects: That the existence required by the judgment is considered only in the state of judgment; unlike the existence required by the predication belonging to the subject, for it is considered always or at a time.
Third of the respects: That the existence required by the judgment is according to the mind; unlike the existence required by the predication belonging to the subject, for it is according to the external sometimes and according to the mind other times.
His saying: "The real" - Know that between the real and the external there is generality in one respect: the external is unique in when you say 'Every color is whiteness' when there is no color except it, and the real is unique in 'Every phoenix is a bird', and they meet in 'Every human is an animal'; so it is real in one respect and external in another.
Al-Attar:
[Commenting on] 'the impossible of the impossibilities'; and if not considered as per the implication of some of their statements, the intended meaning of the assumed existence is that the subject is such that if it existed, it would be united with the predicate. End. His saying: 'The real' - It is named thus because it is the true proposition used in the sciences due to its frequent use in this respect, so it is of the type of attributing a thing to its concept which is like its reality. The mental is named thus because there is no existence for its subject except in the mind.
'Abd al-Hakim said: Know that mental propositions are of divisions; among them are those whose individuals exist in the mind, described by their predicates in the mind with a description matching reality, collecting the logical issues; for their predicates are mental accidents that occur to the primary intelligibles in the mind, and their subjects have two mental existences: one of them is the criterion of judgment, and it is the shadowy existence.
As for the relationship between what exists in the external and what exists in the mind itself, it is absolute generality; because everything existing in the external exists in the mind itself, i.e. in its own essence; so it is a manifestation in the place of implicitness, i.e. disregarding the consideration of the considerer and the supposition of the supposer; whether it exists in the external or not; so therefore existence in the external does not follow from it. Their conjunction: Allah Almighty and Majestic, for He exists in the external, such that the sight may see Him, and in the mind itself in the aforementioned sense, so this is the matter of conjunction.
Example: As for the possibility of events, it exists in the mind itself only; because it is not perceived. As for the relationship between what exists in the external and in the mind, it is generality in one respect. So Zayd: truth applies to him.
Al-Attar:
[The existence] by which the subject and predicate differ, and the second is the original existence by which the predicate unites with the subject, and it is the criterion of truth and falsehood, and the differentiator between the affirmative and the negative. And among them are those whose predicates entail existence; like: 'The partner of Allah is impossible', 'The conjunction of two opposites is impossible', 'The absolutely unknown cannot be judged upon', 'The absolutely non-existent is opposite to the absolutely existent'. And its verification: That the criterion of judgment is their conception under the title of the subject, and the criterion of truth is the hypothetical existence by which the individuality of the subject is considered, as if it were said: 'What is conceived under the title of the partner of Allah and supposed to be true of it is impossible in the mind itself.' And analogize to that. And among them are those whose predicates precede existence, or are existence itself; like: 'Zayd is possible' or 'necessary through another' or 'existent'; so their subjects have existence in the mind at the time of judgment like other propositions. And because the description with them is mental and abstractive, it is necessary that their subjects have another existence in the mind, which is the starting point for abstracting these matters and the criterion of truth of the proposition and the union of the predicates with them. Then, when the intellect turns to them and observes them from the aspect that they are existent with this existence, it abstracts from them existence, possibility, and another existence; considering that description with this existence requires the precedence of an existence that is a referent for these judgments. And this observation is not necessary for the mind always, so it ceases according to the cessation of the observation. End. He said: And it is among the subtleties.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the classification of propositions into real (ḥaqīqiyya), external (khārijiyya), and mental (dhihniyya). It begins by clarifying that the existence of the subject in a proposition can be considered in two ways: from the perspective of the judgment itself (which is always mental) and from the perspective of the actual predication (which may be external or mental). The author then lists three differences between these two types of existence. The first difference: the existence required by judgment occurs in both affirmative and negative propositions, whereas the existence required by predication only occurs in affirmatives. The second difference: the existence required by judgment is considered only at the moment of judgment, while the existence required by predication is considered perpetually or at a specific time. The third difference: the existence required by judgment is mental, while the existence required by predication can be either external or mental.

Next, the author explains the relationship between ‘real’ and ‘external’ propositions. A real proposition is one whose truth is considered regardless of whether its subject actually exists; it only requires that if the subject existed, it would be united with the predicate. An external proposition requires the actual existence of the subject in the external world. The author illustrates this with examples: 'Every color is whiteness' (if only one color existed) is external; 'Every phoenix is a bird' (no actual phoenix) is real; 'Every human is an animal' is both real and external depending on perspective.

The commentary then turns to mental propositions, whose subjects have existence only in the mind. Building on the explanation of ‘Abd al-Ḥakīm, the author divides mental propositions into several types. First, those whose individuals exist in the mind and are described by their predicates in a way that matches reality—these are the logical propositions themselves. Second, those whose predicates entail existence, such as 'The partner of Allah is impossible'; here the criterion of truth is a hypothetical existence. Third, those whose predicates are dispositions or existence itself, e.g., 'Zayd is possible'; such propositions require that the subject have a mental existence that serves as the basis for abstracting the predicate. The author then explains that the intellect can observe this mental existence and abstract from it further attributes like possibility. This observation is not continuous, so the propositions are only true when the mind attends to them.

All examples in this passage are from the Islamic intellectual tradition: the impossibility of a partner to Allah, the impossibility of the conjunction of opposites, and logical concepts like possibility and necessity.

Key Terms
وجود الحكم
وجود ثبوت المحمول

According to al-Dasuqi, which of the following is true about the existence required by the judgment?

B) It is considered always.
C) It is mental.
D) It is external.

Key Takeaway
The existence required by the judgment is always mental and present in both affirmative and negative propositions, while the existence required by the predication is only in affirmatives and can be external or mental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Propositions
beginner

Real, External, and Mental Propositions
The author defines and gives examples of real, external, and mental propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between real (ḥaqīqiyya), external (khārijiyya), and mental (dhihniyya) propositions using their defining characteristics and examples.

Core Idea
A proposition's truth depends on whether its subject's existence is actual, potential, or purely mental.

Having understood the distinction between two types of subject existence, we now turn to the main classification: propositions are either real (ḥaqīqiyya), external (khārijiyya), or mental (dhihniyya). Each type has a distinct condition for its truth. The passage provides examples like 'Every phoenix is a bird' (real) and 'Every color is whiteness' (external), and explains that mental propositions are those whose subjects exist only in the mind.

Guided Path
1
Define each type
Read the passage and extract the definition of each proposition type: real (true regardless of external existence), external (requires external existence), mental (subject exists only in mind).
The passage explicitly states these conditions.
2
Analyze the examples
Consider the examples given: 'Every human is an animal', 'Every phoenix is a bird', 'Every color is whiteness'. Determine which type each belongs to and why.
The example of 'Every color is whiteness' is external only if there is only one color; otherwise it could be real.
3
Understand the relationship between real and external
The passage says real and external have 'generality in one respect'. Draw a Venn diagram showing their overlap.
They overlap in cases like 'Every human is an animal' when humans exist.
4
Identify mental propositions
Find the description of mental propositions. Note that their subjects have existence only in the mind, and they include propositions about impossible entities or logical truths.
The passage mentions examples like 'The partner of Allah is impossible'.

فَالحَقِيقِيَّةُ، أَوْ ذِهْنَاً فَالذِّهْنِيَّةُ).
الدسوقي
والحاصل : أنَّ وجودَ الموضوع تارةً؛ يُعتبرُ مِن حيث الحكم عليه، وتارة؛ يُعتبر من حيث ثبوت المحمول له والوجودُ الَّذي يقتضيه الحكم مخالف للوجود الَّذي يقتضيه ثبوت المحمول للموضوع من أوجه:
الأول : أنَّ الوجود الأوَّلَ يكون في الموجبة والسالبة، والوجود الثاني: لا يكون إلا في الموجبة فقط . الثَّاني من الأوجُهِ : أَنَّ الوجودَ الَّذي يقتضيه الحكمُ إِنَّما يُعتبرُ في حالةِ الحكم فقط؛ بخلافِ الوجودِ الَّذي يقتضيه ثبوتُ المحمول للموضوع، فإِنَّهُ يُعتبرُ دائماً أو ساعة.
الثَّالثُ مِنَ الأومُجهِ : أَنَّ الوجودَ الَّذي يقتضيه الحكم يكون بحسب الذهنِ؛ بخلافِ الوجودِ الَّذي يقتضيه ثبوت المحمول للموضوع، فإنَّه يكون بحسب الخارج تارةً، وبحسب الذهن أُخرى.
قَوْلُهُ : فَالْحَقِيْقَيَّةُ) اعلَمْ أنَّ بين الحقيقيَّةِ والخارجيَّةِ عموماً من وجه، تنفرد الخارجيَّةُ فيما إذا قُلْتَ: كلُّ لون بياضُ، فيما إذا لم يكنْ مِنَ الألوان إلا هو، وتنفرد الحقيقية في: كلُّ عنقاء طائر، ويجتمعان في : كلّ إنسان حيوان، فهي حقيقيَّةٌ باعتبار، وخارجيَّة باعتبار .
العطار
غير الممكنِ مِنَ الممتنعاتِ ؛ وإِنْ لم يعتبرُ كَمَا هو مُقتَضَى كلام بعضِهِم، فَالمراد بالوجودِ المقدَّرِ كونُ الموضوعِ بحيثُ لو وُجِدَ؛ كَانَ مُتَّحداً مع المحمول ا . هـ . قَوْلُهُ : فَالحَقِيقِيَّةُ) سُمِّيَتْ بذلك؛ لأنَّها حقيقةُ القضيَّة المستعملة في العلوم لكثرة استعمالها بهذا الاعتبار، فهو من قبيل نسبةِ الشَّيء إلى مفهومِهِ الَّذي هو كَالحقيقة له، والذهنيَّةُ سُمِّيَتْ بِذلِكَ ؛ لأنَّهُ لَا وجود لموضوعِهَا إِلَّا في الذهنِ، قالَ عبد الحكيم: وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ القضايا الذهنيَّةَ على أقسام ؛ مِنها ما تكونُ أفرادُهَا مَوجودةً في الذهنِ مُتَّصِفَةٌ بمحمولاتها في الذهنِ انصافاً مُطابقاً للواقعِ تَجميعِ المسائل المنطقية؛ فإنَّ محمولاتها عوارض ذهنيَّةً تعرضُ للمعقولات الأولى في الذهن، ويكون لموضوعاتها وُجودانِ ذهنيان؛ أحدهما : مناط الحكم، وهو الوجودُ الظُّلي
وأمَّا النسبة بين الموجود في الخارج والموجود في نَفْسِ الأمرِ؛ فالعموم المطلق؛ لأنَّ كلَّ موجود في الخارج ؛ موجودٌ في نَفْسِ الأمرِ؛ أي : في نَفْسِهِ؛ فهو إظهارٌ في موضع الإضمار ؛ أي : بقطع النَّظرِ عن اعتبار المعتبر وفرض الفارض؛ سواء وجد في الخارج أم لا ؛ فَلِذَا لَا يلزم منه الوجود في الخارج، اجتماعِهِمَا : اللهُ عزَّ وجلَّ، فإنَّهُ مَوجودٌ في الخارج، بحيثُ يجوز رؤيته بالبصر، وفي نَفْسِ الأمرِ بالمعنى المتقدّم، فهذهِ مادَّةُ الاجتماع .
فَمَثَالُ
وأما إمكانُ الحوادث؛ فهو موجودٌ في نَفْسِ الأمر فقط؛ لأنَّهُ لا يُشاهَدُ، وأمَّا النّسبةُ بينَ الموجود في الخارج وفي الدّهنِ؛ فَعموم من وجه، فزيد: يصدقُ عليه
العطار
الَّذِي بِهِ يتغايرُ الموضوعُ والمحمول، وثانيهما: الوجود الأصلي الَّذِي بِهِ اتِّحَادُ المحمول بالموضوع، وهو مناط الصدق والكذب، والفارقُ بين الموجبة والسالبة، ومنها ما تكون محمولاتُها مُستلزمةٌ لِلوجودِ ؛ نحو: شريك الباري ممتنع، واجتماع النَّقيضين مُحالٌ، والمجهول المطلقُ يمتنع الحكم عليه، والمعدوم المطلقُ مُقابل للموجودِ المطلق، وتحقيقه : أنَّ مناط الحكم هو تَصوُّرُها بعنوان الموضوع، ومناط الصدق هو الوجود الفرضيُّ الَّذي باعتبارِ فرديَّةِ الموضوع كأنَّهُ قيلَ: مَا يُتصوَّرُ بعنوان شريكِ الباري ويُفرضُ صدقُهُ عليهِ؛ مُمتنع في نفس الأمرِ، وَقِش على ذلك، ومنها ما تكونُ مَحمولاتُها مُتقدِّمةً على الوجودِ، أو نفس الوجودِ؛ نحو: زيد ممكن أو واجب بالغيرِ أو موجودٌ ؛ فَلِمَوضوعاتِها وجودٌ في الذهنِ حال الحكم كسائر القضايا، وَلِكَوْنِ الاتِّصافِ بها ذهنيّا انتزاعيًّا؛ لا بُدَّ أنْ يكونَ لِمَوضوعاتِها وجودٌ آخَرُ في الذهنِ؛ يكون مبداً لانتزاع هذه الأمور ومناط صدقِ القضيَّةِ واتحاد المحمولات معها ، ثمَّ إذا توجّه العقلُ إليها ولاحظها من حيثُ إِنَّها مَوجودةٌ بهذا الوجودِ؛ انتزع عنها وجوداً وإمكاناً ووجوداً آخر؛ باعتبار الاتصاف بهذا الوجودِ يستدعي تقدم وجود يكون مصداقاً لهذه الأحكام، وليست هذه الملاحظة لازمةٌ للذهن دائماً، فتنقطع بحسب انقطاع الملاحظة ا.هـ. قالَ: وَهوَ مِنَ الغوامض .
Section: On the definition and its divisions.
The real, or mental, then the mental.
Al-Dasuqi:
The summary: That the existence of the subject is sometimes considered from the aspect of the judgment upon it, and sometimes considered from the aspect of the predication belonging to it, and the existence required by the judgment is different from the existence required by the predication belonging to the subject in respects:
First: That the first existence occurs in the affirmative and the negative, while the second existence occurs only in the affirmative alone. Second of the respects: That the existence required by the judgment is considered only in the state of judgment; unlike the existence required by the predication belonging to the subject, for it is considered always or at a time.
Third of the respects: That the existence required by the judgment is according to the mind; unlike the existence required by the predication belonging to the subject, for it is according to the external sometimes and according to the mind other times.
His saying: "The real" - Know that between the real and the external there is generality in one respect: the external is unique in when you say 'Every color is whiteness' when there is no color except it, and the real is unique in 'Every phoenix is a bird', and they meet in 'Every human is an animal'; so it is real in one respect and external in another.
Al-Attar:
[Commenting on] 'the impossible of the impossibilities'; and if not considered as per the implication of some of their statements, the intended meaning of the assumed existence is that the subject is such that if it existed, it would be united with the predicate. End. His saying: 'The real' - It is named thus because it is the true proposition used in the sciences due to its frequent use in this respect, so it is of the type of attributing a thing to its concept which is like its reality. The mental is named thus because there is no existence for its subject except in the mind.
'Abd al-Hakim said: Know that mental propositions are of divisions; among them are those whose individuals exist in the mind, described by their predicates in the mind with a description matching reality, collecting the logical issues; for their predicates are mental accidents that occur to the primary intelligibles in the mind, and their subjects have two mental existences: one of them is the criterion of judgment, and it is the shadowy existence.
As for the relationship between what exists in the external and what exists in the mind itself, it is absolute generality; because everything existing in the external exists in the mind itself, i.e. in its own essence; so it is a manifestation in the place of implicitness, i.e. disregarding the consideration of the considerer and the supposition of the supposer; whether it exists in the external or not; so therefore existence in the external does not follow from it. Their conjunction: Allah Almighty and Majestic, for He exists in the external, such that the sight may see Him, and in the mind itself in the aforementioned sense, so this is the matter of conjunction.
Example: As for the possibility of events, it exists in the mind itself only; because it is not perceived. As for the relationship between what exists in the external and in the mind, it is generality in one respect. So Zayd: truth applies to him.
Al-Attar:
[The existence] by which the subject and predicate differ, and the second is the original existence by which the predicate unites with the subject, and it is the criterion of truth and falsehood, and the differentiator between the affirmative and the negative. And among them are those whose predicates entail existence; like: 'The partner of Allah is impossible', 'The conjunction of two opposites is impossible', 'The absolutely unknown cannot be judged upon', 'The absolutely non-existent is opposite to the absolutely existent'. And its verification: That the criterion of judgment is their conception under the title of the subject, and the criterion of truth is the hypothetical existence by which the individuality of the subject is considered, as if it were said: 'What is conceived under the title of the partner of Allah and supposed to be true of it is impossible in the mind itself.' And analogize to that. And among them are those whose predicates precede existence, or are existence itself; like: 'Zayd is possible' or 'necessary through another' or 'existent'; so their subjects have existence in the mind at the time of judgment like other propositions. And because the description with them is mental and abstractive, it is necessary that their subjects have another existence in the mind, which is the starting point for abstracting these matters and the criterion of truth of the proposition and the union of the predicates with them. Then, when the intellect turns to them and observes them from the aspect that they are existent with this existence, it abstracts from them existence, possibility, and another existence; considering that description with this existence requires the precedence of an existence that is a referent for these judgments. And this observation is not necessary for the mind always, so it ceases according to the cessation of the observation. End. He said: And it is among the subtleties.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the classification of propositions into real (ḥaqīqiyya), external (khārijiyya), and mental (dhihniyya). It begins by clarifying that the existence of the subject in a proposition can be considered in two ways: from the perspective of the judgment itself (which is always mental) and from the perspective of the actual predication (which may be external or mental). The author then lists three differences between these two types of existence. The first difference: the existence required by judgment occurs in both affirmative and negative propositions, whereas the existence required by predication only occurs in affirmatives. The second difference: the existence required by judgment is considered only at the moment of judgment, while the existence required by predication is considered perpetually or at a specific time. The third difference: the existence required by judgment is mental, while the existence required by predication can be either external or mental.

Next, the author explains the relationship between ‘real’ and ‘external’ propositions. A real proposition is one whose truth is considered regardless of whether its subject actually exists; it only requires that if the subject existed, it would be united with the predicate. An external proposition requires the actual existence of the subject in the external world. The author illustrates this with examples: 'Every color is whiteness' (if only one color existed) is external; 'Every phoenix is a bird' (no actual phoenix) is real; 'Every human is an animal' is both real and external depending on perspective.

The commentary then turns to mental propositions, whose subjects have existence only in the mind. Building on the explanation of ‘Abd al-Ḥakīm, the author divides mental propositions into several types. First, those whose individuals exist in the mind and are described by their predicates in a way that matches reality—these are the logical propositions themselves. Second, those whose predicates entail existence, such as 'The partner of Allah is impossible'; here the criterion of truth is a hypothetical existence. Third, those whose predicates are dispositions or existence itself, e.g., 'Zayd is possible'; such propositions require that the subject have a mental existence that serves as the basis for abstracting the predicate. The author then explains that the intellect can observe this mental existence and abstract from it further attributes like possibility. This observation is not continuous, so the propositions are only true when the mind attends to them.

All examples in this passage are from the Islamic intellectual tradition: the impossibility of a partner to Allah, the impossibility of the conjunction of opposites, and logical concepts like possibility and necessity.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a purely real (ḥaqīqiyya) proposition?
A) Every human is an animal (assuming humans exist externally).
B) Every phoenix is a bird (assuming phoenixes do not exist externally).
C) Every color is whiteness (assuming only one color exists).
D) Zayd is possible (Zayd is a specific person).

Key Takeaway
A proposition is real if it is true under the assumption that its subject existed, regardless of actual external existence; external if it requires actual external existence; mental if its subject exists only in the mind.

Propositions
intermediate
7 min
Two Aspects of Every Proposition: Difference and Unity
Author introduces two aspects of any proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two aspects (difference and unity) in any categorical proposition and locate the criterion of truth (manat al-sidq).

Core Idea
Every proposition has a face of difference and a face of unity; truth lies in the unity.

This passage teaches you to see any proposition from two perspectives: the distinction between subject and predicate that makes judgment possible, and the deeper existential unity that makes the judgment true. Understanding this duality is the key to evaluating propositions in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
Take any proposition like 'Zayd is standing'. The subject is 'Zayd' and the predicate is 'standing'. They are different concepts otherwise there would be no judgment.
2
Notice the difference aspect
The predicate is not identical to the subject; they are distinct. This distinction is what allows us to affirm or deny something of the subject.
The author calls this the 'shared attribute' (ما به يتغاير الموضوع والمحمول).
3
Find the unity aspect
Despite conceptual difference, in reality the predicate is united with the subject through existence. For 'Zayd is standing', the standing actually exists in Zayd. This existential union is the basis for truth.
The author calls this 'the original existence by which the predicate unites with the subject'.
4
Define the criterion of truth
The original existence that unites predicate with subject is precisely what makes a proposition true or false. If the union exists, it is true; if not, false.
This is 'manat al-sidq wa al-kadhib'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إمكان الحوادث؛ فهو موجودٌ في نَفْسِ الأمر فقط؛ لأنَّهُ لا يُشاهَدُ، وأمَّا النّسبةُ بينَ الموجود في الخارج وفي الدّهنِ؛ فَعموم من وجه، فزيد: يصدق عليه الَّذِي بِهِ يتغايرُ الموضوعُ والمحمول، وثانيهما: الوجود الأصلي الَّذِي بِهِ اتِّحَادُ المحمول بالموضوع، وهو مناط الصدق والكذب، والفارقُ بين الموجبة والسالبة، ومنها ما تكون محمولاتُها مُستلزمةٌ لِلوجودِ؛ نحو: شريك الباري ممتنع، واجتماع النَّقيضين مُحالٌ، والمجهول المطلقُ يمتنع الحكم عليه، والمعدوم المطلقُ مُقابل للموجودِ المطلق، وتحقيقه: أنَّ مناط الحكم هو تَصوُّرُها بعنوان الموضوع، ومناط الصدق هو الوجود الفرضيُّ الَّذي باعتبارِ فرديَّةِ الموضوع كأنَّهُ قيلَ: مَا يُتصوَّرُ بعنوان شريكِ الباري ويُفرضُ صدقُهُ عليهِ؛ مُمتنع في نفس الأمرِ، وَقِش على ذلك، ومنها ما تكونُ مَحمولاتُها مُتقدِّمةً على الوجودِ، أو نفس الوجودِ؛ نحو: زيد ممكن أو واجب بالغيرِ أو موجودٌ؛ فَلِمَوضوعاتِها وجودٌ في الذهنِ حال الحكم كسائر القضايا، وَلِكَوْنِ الاتِّصافِ بها ذهنيّا انتزاعيًّا؛ لا بُدَّ أنْ يكونَ لِمَوضوعاتِها وجودٌ آخَرُ في الذهنِ؛ يكون مبداً لانتزاع هذه الأمور ومناط صدقِ القضيَّةِ واتحاد المحمولات معها، ثمَّ إذا توجّه العقلُ إليها ولاحظها من حيثُ إِنَّها مَوجودةٌ بهذا الوجودِ؛ انتزع عنها وجوداً وإمكاناً ووجوداً آخر؛ باعتبار الاتصاف بهذا الوجودِ يستدعي تقدم وجود يكون مصداقاً لهذه الأحكام، وليست هذه الملاحظة لازمةٌ للذهن دائماً، فتنقطع بحسب انقطاع الملاحظة ا.هـ. قالَ: وَهوَ مِنَ الغوامض .
As for the possibility of events (accidents), it exists only in the divine knowledge because it is not observed. As for the relationship between existence in the external world and existence in the mind: it is generality from one perspective. So Zayd: the predicate applies to him in that the subject and predicate are different; and the second is the original existence by which the predicate unites with the subject, and it is the criterion of truth and falsehood, and the distinguisher between affirmative and negative. Among propositions, some have predicates that entail existence: e.g., 'the partner of Allah is impossible', 'the conjunction of two contradictories is impossible', 'the absolutely unknown cannot be judged upon', 'the absolute nonexistent is the opposite of the absolute existent'. Its verification: the criterion of judgment is conceiving them under the description of the subject, and the criterion of truth is the suppositional existence by which the individuality of the subject is considered, as if it were said: 'what is conceived under the description of partner of Allah and supposed to be true of it is impossible in the divine knowledge'. And measure by that. Among them, some have predicates that are prior to existence or existence itself: e.g., 'Zayd is possible or necessary by another or existent'; so their subjects have existence in the mind at the time of judgment like other propositions. And because the attribution is mental and abstract, it is necessary that their subjects have another existence in the mind that is a starting point for abstracting these matters and the criterion of truth of the proposition and the union of the predicates with them. Then when the mind turns to them and observes them as existent by this existence, it abstracts from them existence, possibility, and another existence, because the attribution by this existence requires a prior existence that is a concrete instance of these judgments. And this observation is not always necessary for the mind, so it ceases when the observation ceases. End quote. He said: And this is among the subtle points.
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of propositions (qadaya) based on the relation of their predicates to existence. The author first contrasts existence in the external world and existence in the mind, noting that their relationship is 'generality from one perspective' (umum min wajh), meaning some things exist in both, some only externally (e.g., things under the earth), and some only mentally (e.g., imagining generosity in a miser). He then distinguishes two aspects of a proposition: the difference between subject and predicate (the ground for the judgment) and the unity of predicate with subject via existence (the criterion of truth). Propositions are divided into two types: (1) Those whose predicates entail existence, such as 'the partner of Allah is impossible'. In these, the truth depends on a suppositional existence of the subject in the mind at the time of judgment; the subject need not exist externally. (2) Those whose predicates are prior to existence or are existence itself, such as 'Zayd is possible'. Here the subject must have a mental existence that serves as a basis for abstracting these attributions. The author explains the subtle mechanism: the mind observes the subject as mentally existing, then abstracts properties like possibility or existence from it. This mental observation is not continuous; it ceases when attention stops. The passage concludes by noting that this is a subtle point (min al-ghawamid).

Key Terms
مناط الصدق والكذب
الوجود الأصلي
اتحاد المحمول بالموضوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The subject's external existence
B) The conceptual distinction between subject and predicate
C) The original existence that unites predicate with subject
D) The mental observation of the subject

Key Takeaway
Every proposition has two aspects: the conceptual difference that enables judgment, and the existential unity that grounds truth; the latter is the criterion of truth.

Propositions

Types of Propositions by Predicate Relation to Existence
Author classifies propositions by predicate relation to existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify propositions into those whose predicates entail existence and those whose predicates are prior to existence or existence itself, and explain the role of suppositional existence.

Core Idea
Predicates either demand a supposed existence or rely on mental existence for abstraction.

The passage divides propositions into two major types based on what their predicates require from existence. In one type, the predicate is something impossible or non-existent, so the subject needs only a suppositional existence in the mind. In the other type, the predicate is a property like possibility or existence itself, requiring the subject to have a real mental existence from which the property can be abstracted. This distinction is crucial for understanding how we can make true judgments about non-existent things or about existence itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the predicate type
Look at the predicate of the proposition. Is it something that cannot exist externally, like 'impossible' or 'non-existent'? Or is it something like 'possible' or 'existent'?
Examples: 'Partner of Allah is impossible' – predicate is impossibility; 'Zayd is possible' – predicate is possibility.
2
Classify the proposition
If the predicate entails existence (i.e., it is a negative attribute like impossibility), then the proposition belongs to the first type. If the predicate is prior to existence or existence itself, it belongs to the second type.
The first type: predicates like 'impossible', 'non-existent'. The second: 'possible', 'necessary', 'existent'.
3
Understand the existence condition for each type
For the first type, the truth depends on a suppositional existence (wujud fardi) of the subject in the mind; the subject need not exist externally. For the second type, the subject must have a mental existence that serves as a basis for abstracting the predicate.
Suppositional existence is like imagining a partner for Allah to judge it impossible.
4
Apply the mechanism
For a proposition like 'Zayd is possible', the mind first conceives Zayd as having mental existence, then abstracts possibility from that existence. This abstraction occurs only when the mind actively observes; it ceases when attention stops.
This subtle point is why the author calls it 'min al-ghawamid'.

Source Text

وأما إمكان الحوادث؛ فهو موجودٌ في نَفْسِ الأمر فقط؛ لأنَّهُ لا يُشاهَدُ، وأمَّا النّسبةُ بينَ الموجود في الخارج وفي الدّهنِ؛ فَعموم من وجه، فزيد: يصدق عليه الَّذِي بِهِ يتغايرُ الموضوعُ والمحمول، وثانيهما: الوجود الأصلي الَّذِي بِهِ اتِّحَادُ المحمول بالموضوع، وهو مناط الصدق والكذب، والفارقُ بين الموجبة والسالبة، ومنها ما تكون محمولاتُها مُستلزمةٌ لِلوجودِ؛ نحو: شريك الباري ممتنع، واجتماع النَّقيضين مُحالٌ، والمجهول المطلقُ يمتنع الحكم عليه، والمعدوم المطلقُ مُقابل للموجودِ المطلق، وتحقيقه: أنَّ مناط الحكم هو تَصوُّرُها بعنوان الموضوع، ومناط الصدق هو الوجود الفرضيُّ الَّذي باعتبارِ فرديَّةِ الموضوع كأنَّهُ قيلَ: مَا يُتصوَّرُ بعنوان شريكِ الباري ويُفرضُ صدقُهُ عليهِ؛ مُمتنع في نفس الأمرِ، وَقِش على ذلك، ومنها ما تكونُ مَحمولاتُها مُتقدِّمةً على الوجودِ، أو نفس الوجودِ؛ نحو: زيد ممكن أو واجب بالغيرِ أو موجودٌ؛ فَلِمَوضوعاتِها وجودٌ في الذهنِ حال الحكم كسائر القضايا، وَلِكَوْنِ الاتِّصافِ بها ذهنيّا انتزاعيًّا؛ لا بُدَّ أنْ يكونَ لِمَوضوعاتِها وجودٌ آخَرُ في الذهنِ؛ يكون مبداً لانتزاع هذه الأمور ومناط صدقِ القضيَّةِ واتحاد المحمولات معها، ثمَّ إذا توجّه العقلُ إليها ولاحظها من حيثُ إِنَّها مَوجودةٌ بهذا الوجودِ؛ انتزع عنها وجوداً وإمكاناً ووجوداً آخر؛ باعتبار الاتصاف بهذا الوجودِ يستدعي تقدم وجود يكون مصداقاً لهذه الأحكام، وليست هذه الملاحظة لازمةٌ للذهن دائماً، فتنقطع بحسب انقطاع الملاحظة ا.هـ. قالَ: وَهوَ مِنَ الغوامض .

Negative Propositions Require Mental Subject Existence
Author explains that negative propositions also require the subject's mental existence at the time of judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why negative propositions require the subject to exist in the mind at the time of judgment.

Core Idea
Even negations demand a mental subject; judgment always requires conception.

In previous lessons, you learned that affirmative propositions require subject existence. But what about negative propositions? The author argues that negation is also a judgment, and every judgment requires a conceived subject. Thus, negative propositions too necessitate the subject's mental existence at the moment of judgment. This lesson unpacks that requirement through the primary text and its commentaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Locate the author's statement that negative propositions require the subject's mental existence. Notice the phrase 'تقتضي وجود الموضوع أيضاً في الذهن'.
The 'also' (أيضاً) links to the earlier discussion of affirmative propositions.
2
Understand the reasoning
The author explains that negation is a judgment (حكم), and every judgment requires a conception (تصور) of the subject. So the subject must be present in the mind at the time of judgment.
This reasoning is the core logical move.
3
Distinguish two types of existence
The commentary by al-Attar (quoting al-Sayyid) distinguishes between the existence required for the act of judgment (momentary mental presence) and the existence required for the truth of the proposition (lasting as long as the predicate holds).
For negative propositions, only the former is necessary.
4
Apply to examples
Consider examples from al-Dasuqi: things under the earth exist externally only; imagining generosity in a miser is mental only; unknown divine attributes exist in nafs al-amr only. Identify what existence is present for a negative judgment about each.
This reinforces the concept through concrete cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي المَعَرْفِ وَأَقْسَامِهِ
واعلَمْ أنَّ السَّالبة تقتضي وجود الموضوع أيضاً في الذهن، من حيثُ إِنَّ السَّلْبَ حُكْمٌ، فلا بُدَّ لهُ من تصورِ المحكوم عليه، لكنْ إِنَّما يُعتبر هذا الوجود حال الحكم؛ أي: بمقدار ما يحكم الحاكم بالمحمول على الموضوع، كلحظة مثلاً.
الدسوقي
أنَّهُ مَوجود ذهناً؛ لاستحضاره فيه، وفي الخارج؛ لِمُشاهدتِهِ، وَمَا تحتَ الأرضين موجود في الخارج دون الذهنِ، ومثال انفراد الوجودِ الذهني: استحضار كرمِ شخص بخيل، فهذا وجود ذهني لا خارجي
وأمَّا النِّسبةُ الَّتي بينَ الموجودِ الذَّهني وفي نَفْسِ الأمر؛ فوجهي أيضاً، فإمكانُ زيد موجودٌ في نَفْسِ الأمرِ وَفِي الذَّهنِ، وانفراد الذهني باستحضارك كرم البخيل. ومثال انفرادِ الموجودِ في نَفْسِ الأمرِ: صفاتُ اللهِ الكماليَّةُ الَّتي لم نطَّلِعْ عليها، فهذهِ مَوجودةٌ في نَفْسِ الأمرِ دونَ الذهن؛ إذ الفرضُ أَنَّها لم تخطر بالبال. (قَوْلُهُ: أَيْضَاً) أي: كَمَا تقتضيه الموجبة.
(قَوْلُهُ: فِي الذَّهْنِ) مُتعلّق بوجود.
(قَوْلُهُ: الْمَحْكُوْم عَلَيْهِ) أي: الموضوع.
(قَوْلُهُ: حَالَ الْحُكْمِ أي: وقتَ الحُكْمِ.
(قَوْلُهُ: أَيْ: بِمِقْدَارِ بيان لحالِ الحُكْمِ.
(قَوْلُهُ: كَلَحْظَةٍ) بيان للمقدارِ.
العطار
(قَوْلُهُ: وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ السَّالِبَةَ ... إِلَخ) مرتبط بقولِهِ: (وَلَا بُدَّ في الموجبة من وجود الموضوع)، وما ذُكِرَ مَأخوذُ مِن قولِ السَّيِّدِ: الإيجاب يقتضي وجود الموضوع في الذهنِ مِن حيثُ إِنَّهُ حكمٌ، فَلَا بُدَّ لَهُ مِن تَصوُّرِ المحكوم عليه، ويقتضي صدقه ووجودَهُ أيضاً؛ لأنَّ ثبوت المحمول له فرع ثبوته في نفسه، والفرق بين هَذَينِ الوجودين؛ أنَّ الوجودَ الَّذي يقتضيه الحكمُ إِنَّما يُعتبرُ حال الحكم؛ أي: بمقدار ما يحكم الحاكم بالمحمول على الموضوعِ كَلَحظة مثلاً، وأنَّ الوجودَ الَّذي يقتضيه ثبوت المحمول للموضوع هو بحسب ثبوتِهِ: إِنْ دائماً؛ فَدائماً، وإن ساعةً؛ فَساعة
Section: On Definition and Its Divisions
And know that the negative proposition also requires the existence of the subject in the mind, because negation is a judgment, so it must have a conception of the thing judged upon. However, this existence is considered only at the time of judgment, i.e., to the extent that the judge judges the predicate of the subject, like a moment, for example.
Al-Dasuqi: That it exists mentally, because it is present in the mind, and externally, because it is observed. And what is under the earth exists externally without the mind. An example of mental existence alone: the imagery of generosity from a miser; this is mental existence, not external.
As for the relationship between mental existence and existence in the divine knowledge: it is also from one perspective. So the possibility of Zayd exists in the divine knowledge and in the mind, and the mental alone is your imagery of a miser's generosity. An example of existence in the divine knowledge alone: the perfect attributes of Allah that we have not come to know; these exist in the divine knowledge without the mind, because it is assumed they did not cross the mind.
(His statement: 'also') Means: just as the affirmative requires it.
(His statement: 'in the mind') Attached to 'existence'.
(His statement: 'the thing judged upon') Means the subject.
(His statement: 'at the time of judgment') Means the time of judgment.
(His statement: 'i.e., to the extent') An explanation of the situation of judgment.
(His statement: 'like a moment') An explanation of the extent.
Al-Attar: (His statement: 'And know that the negative... etc.') is connected to his statement: 'And there is no need in the affirmative for the existence of the subject', and what was mentioned is taken from the statement of al-Sayyid: 'The affirmative requires the existence of the subject in the mind in that it is a judgment, so it must have a conception of the thing judged upon, and it also requires its truth and its existence, because the establishment of the predicate for it is a branch of its establishment in itself. The difference between these two existences is that the existence required by the judgment is considered only at the time of judgment, i.e., to the extent that the judge judges the predicate of the subject, like a moment, for example; and that the existence required by the establishment of the predicate to the subject is according to its establishment: if permanent, then permanent; if momentary, then momentary.'
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage, beginning with a new section heading 'On Definition and Its Divisions' (though the content continues on negative propositions), explains that negative propositions also require the subject's mental existence at the time of judgment. The author states that negation is a judgment (hukm), so there must be a conception (tasawwur) of the subject; hence the subject must exist in the mind while the judgment is made, but only momentarily. Al-Dasuqi's commentary elaborates: things can exist mentally (by being present in the mind), externally (by observation), or both. For example, things under the earth exist externally only, while imagining generosity in a miser is mental only. The relationship between mental existence and existence in nafs al-amr is 'from one perspective' (wajhi): some things exist in both (like Zayd's possibility), some only mentally (like the miser's generosity), some only in nafs al-amr (like unknown divine attributes). The text then parses phrases from the original matn, clarifying that 'also' connects to the affirmative, 'in the mind' modifies 'existence', etc. Al-Attar adds that this discussion links to the earlier claim that affirmatives require subject existence in the mind, citing al-Sayyid's distinction: the existence required for judgment (momentary mental presence) versus the existence required for truth (which lasts as long as the predicate holds). Thus, even negative propositions necessitate a minimal mental existence of the subject for the judgment to occur.

Key Terms
سالبة (saliba): A negative proposition that denies a predicate of a subject; it requires the subject to exist in the mind at the time of judgment. [Note: Standard term; no significant shift.]
وجود ذهني (wujud dhihni): Mental existence, i.e., the subject is present in the mind as a concept, even if not externally real. [Note: In earlier falsafa, mental existence was often considered a mode of being; here it is used practically for judgment analysis.]
حكم (hukm): A judgment, which in logic is the act of affirming or denying a predicate of a subject; it necessitates a conception of the subject. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, hukm refers to a legal ruling; here it is purely logical.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the predicate cannot be denied unless the subject is real
D) Because the proposition must be true

Key Takeaway
Negative propositions, being judgments, necessitate a conception of the subject, hence the subject must have mental existence at the time of judgment.

Propositions

Subject Existence in Propositions: Mental vs Real
Commentators explain the two types of subject existence required in affirmative and negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni) and real existence (al-wujud al-khariji) and explain why a negative proposition requires mental existence of its subject.

Core Idea
A negative judgment still needs a subject in the mind — you cannot deny what you have not conceived.

In logic, every proposition involves a judgment (hukm) about a subject. You might think that a negative proposition like 'Zayd is not standing' only needs the subject to exist in reality for the denial to make sense. But the scholars of mantiq argue that even negation is a judgment, and every judgment requires the subject to be present in the mind at the moment of judgment. This leads to a deeper point: there are two kinds of existence — mental and real — and a proposition's requirement for each differs depending on its type. Let's unpack this passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about negative propositions
The passage states that a negative proposition (saliba) also requires existence of the subject in the mind (wujud dhihni). Note: This is parallel to the affirmative's requirement.
The word 'also' in the text indicates this parallels the affirmative case.
2
Understand the reasoning: negation is a judgment
Because negation is a judgment (hukm), and every judgment requires a conception (tasawwur) of the subject judged upon. Without a mental concept, there can be no judgment.
This is a fundamental principle in Islamic logic.
3
Determine when mental existence is required
The passage specifies that it is only required at the time of judgment (hal al-hukm) and to the extent needed to predicate the predicate, like a fleeting moment.
This avoids the requirement of permanent mental existence.
4
Distinguish between mental and real existence
The commentator gives examples: the possibility of Zayd exists both mentally and in reality; a miser's generosity exists only mentally; Allah's unknown attributes exist only in reality.
This clarifies the two ontological domains: mental (dhihni) and real (fi nafs al-amr).
5
Apply the distinction to the negative proposition
For a negative proposition to be meaningful, the subject must exist in the mind at judgment. It need not exist in reality (e.g., 'The phoenix does not exist' is meaningful because we have a mental concept of phoenix).
This is the practical upshot for evaluating propositions.
