وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ السَّالِبَةَ تَقْتَضِي وُجُودَ الْمَوْضُوعِ أَيْضاً فِي الذِّهْنِ، مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّ السَّلْبَ حُكْمٌ، فَلَا بُدَّ لَهُ مِنْ تَصَوُّرِ الْمَحْكُومِ عَلَيْهِ. لَكِنْ إِنَّمَا يُعْتَبَرُ هَذَا الْوُجُودُ حَالَ الْحُكْمِ؛ أَيْ: بِمِقْدَارِ مَا يَحْكُمُ الْحَاكِمُ بِالْمَحْمُولِ عَلَى الْمَوْضُوعِ، كَلَحْظَةٍ مَثَلاً. وَأَمَّا النِّسْبَةُ الَّتِي بَيْنَ الْمَوْجُودِ الذِّهْنِيِّ وَفِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ؛ فَوُجُودِيٌّ أَيْضاً، فَإِمْكَانُ زَيْدٍ مَوْجُودٌ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ وَفِي الذِّهْنِ، وَانْفِرَادُ الذِّهْنِيِّ بِاسْتِحْضَارِكَ كَرَمَ الْبَخِيلِ. وَمِثَالُ انْفِرَادِ الْمَوْجُودِ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ: صِفَاتُ اللَّهِ الْكَمَالِيَّةُ الَّتِي لَمْ نَطَّلِعْ عَلَيْهَا، فَهَذِهِ مَوْجُودَةٌ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ دُونَ الذِّهْنِ؛ إِذِ الْفَرْضُ أَنَّهَا لَمْ تَخْطُرْ بِالْبَالِ. (قَوْلُهُ: أَيْضاً) أَيْ: كَمَا تَقْتَضِيهِ الْمُوجِبَةُ. (قَوْلُهُ: فِي الذِّهْنِ) مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِوُجُودٍ. (قَوْلُهُ: الْمَحْكُومِ عَلَيْهِ) أَيْ: الْمَوْضُوعِ. (قَوْلُهُ: حَالَ الْحُكْمِ) أَيْ: وَقْتَ الْحُكْمِ. (قَوْلُهُ: أَيْ: بِمِقْدَارِ) بَيَانٌ لِحَالِ الْحُكْمِ. (قَوْلُهُ: كَلَحْظَةٍ) بَيَانٌ لِلْمِقْدَارِ.
And know that the negative proposition also requires the existence of the subject in the mind, because negation is a judgment, so it must have a conception of that which is judged upon. But this existence is only considered at the time of judgment; i.e., to the extent that the judge judges the predicate of the subject, like a moment for example. As for the relation between mental existence and existence in reality, it is also existential; so the possibility of Zayd exists in reality and in the mind, and the mental existence is unique in your imagining the generosity of a miser. An example of existence solely in reality: the perfect attributes of Allah which we have not come to know; these exist in reality without the mind, since it is assumed they have not occurred to thought. (His saying: 'also') i.e., just as the affirmative requires it. (His saying: 'in the mind') is attached to 'existence'. (His saying: 'that which is judged upon') i.e., the subject. (His saying: 'at the time of judgment') i.e., the time of judgment. (His saying: 'i.e., to the extent') is an explanation of the state of judgment. (His saying: 'like a moment') is an explanation of the extent.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage opens a discussion on the requirements for subject existence in categorical propositions. The author of the original matn states that a negative proposition also requires the existence of the subject in the mind, because negation is a judgment (ḥukm), and every judgment requires a conception of the subject (the thing judged upon). The existence of the subject in the mind is only required at the moment of judging – to the extent that the judge attributes the predicate to the subject, like a fleeting moment. The commentator then clarifies the distinction between two types of existence: mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni) and real existence (al-wujud fi nafs al-amr). They are both 'existential' in the sense that they are real in their own domains. For example, the possibility of Zayd exists both in reality (as a potential) and in the mind (as a concept). But mental existence alone is illustrated by thinking of a miser's generosity – that idea exists only in the mind, not in external reality. Real existence alone is exemplified by Allah's perfect attributes that we have not discovered; they exist in reality but not in our minds since we have not conceived them. The glosses then break down the key phrases: 'also' means the negative proposition requires it just like the affirmative; 'in the mind' qualifies the type of existence; 'that which is judged upon' is the subject; 'at the time of judgment' specifies when; 'to the extent' and 'like a moment' clarify the minimal duration of mental existence needed for the judgment.

Key Terms
سالبة
وجود ذهني
وجود خارجي
حكم

Checkpoint
Why does a negative proposition (e.g., 'Zayd is not standing') require the existence of the subject in the mind?
A) Because negation is a judgment, and every judgment needs a conceived subject.
B) Because the subject must exist in reality for the proposition to be meaningful.
C) Because negation implies the absence of the subject.
D) Because the predicate 'standing' must exist in the mind.

Key Takeaway
A negative proposition requires the subject's mental existence at the time of judgment, because negation is a judgment that depends on conceiving the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'The world is created.' Identify what kind of existence (mental, real, or both) is required for the subject 'the world' in this affirmative proposition. Explain your answer based on the text.
?
Construct a negative proposition about a non-existent entity (e.g., 'The chain of infinite causes does not exist'). Explain why this proposition is meaningful according to the lesson.

Propositions
intermediate
7 min
Two Types of Mental Existence in Propositions
Commentators distinguish two mental existences required for judgment and predicate attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of mental existence (the one required by the judgment and the one required by the predicate's attribution) and identify which type is relevant in a given proposition.

Core Idea
Every proposition requires two mental existences: one for the judgment moment, one for the predicate's hanging onto the subject.

The commentators of the Tahdhib draw a subtle but crucial distinction between two kinds of mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni) involved in propositions. This distinction is not merely theoretical; it determines when an affirmative proposition is true and when a negative proposition suffices. In this lesson, you will learn to identify these two existences and understand why they matter for both positive and negative claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two existences from the text
Look at the beginning of the passage: 'That mental existence which the judgment requires is different from the mental existence which the predicate's attribution to the subject requires.' Notice that the first existence is tied to the act of judgment (hukm), the second to the attribution (thubut al-mahmul).
The first existence is for the judgment itself; the second is for the predicate's relation to the subject.
2
Understand the second existence's correspondence to the mode of attribution
The second existence is not fixed; it varies with the predicate's attribute: if permanent, then permanent; if external, then external; etc. This is clarified in the example of God's existence (eternal) vs. lightning's flash (momentary).
The mode of the attribute (e.g., permanence, temporality) determines the mode of the required existence.
3
Apply the distinction to an Islamic example: 'Allah is omnipotent'
For this affirmative proposition, the first existence is our mental conception of Allah when we make the judgment. The second existence is Allah's actual eternal existence, because the predicate 'omnipotent' holds for Him permanently.
4
Try a negative example: 'No idol is divine'
The first existence is the mental conception of idols at judgment. The second existence is not required because negation does not need the subject to exist; the predicate 'divine' is simply absent.

وذلك الوجودُ الذهني الذي يقتضيه الحكم مغاير للوجودِ الذهني الذي يقتضيه ثبوتُ المحمول للموضوع، فإنَّ الوجودَ الثَّانِيَ إِنَّما يُعتبرُ بحسَبِ ثبوتِ المحمول للموضوع، إن دائماً فدائماً، وإن خارجاً فخارجاً، وإن ذهناً فذهناً، وإن لحظةً فَلحظة، والسَّالبة تشارك الموجبة في اقتضاء الوجودِ الأَوَّلِ دونَ الثَّاني، وكذا الحال في الفرق بين الموجبة والسالبة إذا أخذَتْ ذهنيَّة .ا.هـ. وقيَّد المصنِّفُ في شرح الرّسالة اقتضاء الموجبة وجود الموضوع بما إذا كانَتْ خارجيَّةً أو حقيقيَّةً، وأمَّا الذّهنيّةُ، فَلَا تقتضي إِلَّا تصور الموضوع حال الحكم كَمَا في السَّوالِبِ مِن غير فرق، ولا تفتقر إلى وجودِ الموضوع حال ثبوتِ الحكم، بل لا يصح وجوده في تلك الحالة، والقولُ بأنَّها سوالب في المعنى ممنوع؛ إذ الحكمُ إِنَّما هو بوقوع النّسبة. ا.هـ. أي: والإرجاع إلى السَّلبِ تَعشُفٌ، وردَّهُ عبد الحكيم بأنَّه يهدمُ المقدِّمة البديهيَّةَ الَّتي يُبتنى عليها كثيرٌ مِنَ المسائلِ مِن أنَّ ثبوت شيءٍ لشيء؛ فرغ ثبوتِ المثبتِ له؛ إذ التخصيص لا يجري في القواعد العقليَّةِ. (قَوْلُهُ : إِنْ دَائِمَاً) أي: إنْ كانَ التَّبوتُ المقتضي دائماً؛ فالوجود المتقضي يكون دائماً، وهكذا . وإيضاح الفرق بين الوجودَيْن يظهرُ فيما إذا قُلْنَا : الله تعالى موجودٌ أزلاً وأبداً، موجوده في الذهن لأجلِ الحُكْمِ؛ إنَّما هو حال الإيقاع، وَوُجودُهُ لأجل ثبوتِ المحمول لهُ؛ أزليّ أبديّ. وإذا قيلَ : البرقُ لامع ؛ فَوجود البرقِ في الذهن لأجل الحكم؛ إنَّما حال الحُكْمِ، ووجوده في الخارج لأجل ثبوتِ اللّمعانِ لَهُ فِي لَحظة؛ لَأَنَّ اللَّمَعَانَ إِنَّمَا يثبت للبرق لحظةً. (قَوْلُهُ: وَإِنْ ذِهْنَاً؛ فَذِهْنَاً أي : كَمَا في قولِكَ : شريك الباري معدوم، فَثبوتُ العدم لِلشَّريكِ ذهني، كما أنَّ وجوده ذهني. (قَوْلُهُ : فِى اقْتِضَائِهِ) أي : في اقتضاء كل منهما إيَّاه. (قَوْلُهُ: يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى الْوُجُوْدِ الثَّانِي) أي: وهو الوجود الذي يقتضيه ثبوتُ المحمول للموضوع، فَلَا تصدقُ الموجبة إلا إذا كان موضوعُهَا مَوجوداً؛ لأنَّ ثبوت شَيءٍ لِشَيءٍ؛ يقتضي ثبوت الشَّيء المثبت له. (قَوْلُهُ : بِخِلَافِ السَّالِبَةِ) أي : فإِنَّهُ لا يتوقَّفُ صدقها على الوجودِ الَّذي يقتضيه ثبوتُ سَلْبِ المحمول؛ لأنَّ سَلْبَ المحمول عَنِ الموضوع لا يقتضي وجوده؛ بخلاف ثبوتِه له . ومِنْ هذا قِيلَ : إِنَّ السَّالبة تصدقُ مع نفي الموضوع، والموجبة لا تصدقُ إلا مَعَ وجودِ الموضوع. (قَوْلُهُ : تَأَمَّلْ) أي : في هذا المقامِ لِدِقتِهِ. (قَوْلُهُ : تَأَمَّلْ) أي : حتَّى يظهر لك الفرقُ بين الموجبة والسالبة عند من يرى اطّراد الحكم في سائر الموجبات؛ كَمَا هو المنقولُ عَنِ السَّيِّدِ وغيرِهِ، أَوْ أَنَّ هذا الحكم الواقع
That mental existence which the judgment requires is different from the mental existence which the predicate's attribution to the subject requires. For the second existence is only considered in accordance with the predicate's attribution to the subject: if permanent then permanent, if external then external, if mental then mental, if momentary then momentary. The negative shares with the affirmative in requiring the first existence but not the second. The same is the case in the difference between the affirmative and the negative when considered mentally. End quote. The author in the commentary on the Treatise restricted the affirmative's requirement of the subject's existence to when it is external or real; as for the mental, it requires only the conception of the subject at the time of judgment as in negatives without difference, and it does not need the existence of the subject at the time of the attribute's affirmation; rather its existence in that state is not correct, and the statement that they are negatives in meaning is prohibited because the judgment is only about the occurrence of the relation. End quote. That is: referring it to negation is arbitrary. 'Abd al-Hakim refuted it by saying that it demolishes the self-evident premise on which many issues are built, that the affirmation of something to something requires the affirmation of that to which it is affirmed, for specification does not apply in rational principles. (His saying: 'If permanent') i.e., if the required attribution is permanent, then the required existence is permanent, and so on. And the clarification of the difference between the two existences appears when we say: God is existent eternally and perpetually; His existence in the mind for the sake of judgment is only at the moment of assertion, and His existence for the sake of the attribute's affirmation to Him is eternal and perpetual. And when it is said: lightning flashes; the existence of lightning in the mind for the sake of judgment is only at the moment of judgment, and its existence externally for the sake of the flashing affirmation to it is at a moment, because the flashing is only affirmed to lightning at a moment. (His saying: 'And if mentally, then mentally') i.e., as in your statement: The partner of God is non-existent; so the affirmation of non-existence to the partner is mental, just as its existence is mental. (His saying: 'In its requirement') i.e., in each of them requiring it. (His saying: 'Is dependent on the second existence') i.e., it is the existence that the predicate's attribution to the subject requires. Thus the affirmative is not true unless its subject exists, because the affirmation of something to something requires the existence of that to which it is affirmed. (His saying: 'Unlike the negative') i.e., its truth does not depend on the existence that the denial of the predicate requires, because denying the predicate from the subject does not require its existence, unlike affirming it. Hence it is said: The negative is true with the subject's non-existence, while the affirmative is true only with the subject's existence. (His saying: 'Reflect') i.e., on this matter due to its subtlety. (His saying: 'Reflect') i.e., so that the difference between the affirmative and negative becomes clear to you, according to one who considers the rule to apply to all affirmatives, as is transmitted from al-Sayyid and others, or that this actual ruling...
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a prior matn statement about the distinction between two types of mental existence required in propositions. The commentators (al-Dasuqi and al-'Attar, with a mention of 'Abd al-Hakim) explain that a judgment (hukm) requires a mental existence of the subject at the moment of judgment (al-awwal), while the predicate's attribution to the subject requires a separate mental existence (al-thani) that corresponds to the temporal or modal status of the attribution. The affirmative proposition requires both; the negative only requires the first. The author then discusses an exception for mental propositions, where the affirmative also only requires conception, not actual existence of the subject at the time of the attributive relation. He criticizes those who would reduce such affirmatives to negatives, calling it arbitrary (ta'assuf). 'Abd al-Hakim defends the standard view by invoking the self-evident principle that 'affirmation of something to something requires the existence of that to which it is affirmed.' The passage then provides examples: God exists eternally (affirmative with eternal attribute) and lightning flashes (affirmative with momentary attribute). The second existence for God is eternal; for lightning it is momentary. The third example, 'The partner of God is non-existent,' illustrates a mental affirmative (a shirk denial) where both existence and attribution are mental. The commentary emphasizes that the negative proposition does not require the subject's external existence, unlike the affirmative. The final instruction 'Reflect' (ta'ammal) warns the reader of the subtlety. This analysis uses kalam examples (God's existence, refutation of shirk) and a natural phenomenon (lightning) to ground abstract logical distinctions.

Key Terms
الوجود الذهني
الوجود الذي يقتضيه الحكم
الوجود الذي يقتضيه ثبوت المحمول للموضوع

Checkpoint
According to the commentators, when we say 'Allah is existent eternally', what is the second mental existence?
A) Allah's existence in the mind at judgment
B) Allah's eternal existence
C) The moment of judgment
D) The predicate 'existent'

Key Takeaway
The judgment requires a mental existence of the subject at the moment of assertion; the predicate's attribution requires a separate mental existence that matches the attribute's mode.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider the proposition 'The Prophet's sunna is abrogated by the Qur'an' (an affirmative usuli claim). Identify which two mental existences are required for this proposition to be true. Explain why both are necessary.
?
2. Consider the negative proposition 'No companion of the Prophet is a prophet.' Which mental existence does it require? Why does it not require the second?
